<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

The fading channel problem is often mitigated using diversity combining techniques. In this letter, a novel dual-antenna passive UHF RFID tag with polarization-time coded backscatter diversity is proposed. An RFID system with such kind of tags can use polarization diversity combining technology to overcome the fading problem. The proposed polarization-time coding scheme not only lowers the corresponding RFID tag design complexity, but also effectively reduces detection errors of modulated backscatter signal. Empirical results of the dual-antenna tag emulator validate the feasibility of the proposed design.</div>

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>The applications of passive UHF RFID systems increase rapidly. However, the tag read rate is still unsatisfied in many applications. Particularly in an environment with abundant multiple paths, tag readability is a serious problem due to deep signal fading <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>. Diversity-combining techniques are widely used in state-of-the-art communication systems to mitigate the fading problem. Applying a transmit diversity technique to an RFID system is first investigated in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>. Griffin and Durgin used multiple antennas to create pinhole diversity gains in an RFID system recently <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>, the authors consider that both the RFID reader (including transmitter and receiver) and tag have multiple antennas and do not use any space-time or polarization-time coding techniques. This letter proposes a low-complexity dual-antenna passive tag with polarization-time coded backscatter diversity. A regular RFID system, where only one RFID reader antenna is usually used at a time, should benefit from the backscatter diversity (analogy to a transmit diversity) gain. Experimental results using a dual-antenna tag emulator confirm the feasibility of the proposed design.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Design of Dual-Antenna Passive UHF RFID Tag</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. In the forward (reader-to-tag) link, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-93" role="math" style="width: 5.529em; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-97"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-98"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-99" style="font-size: 70.7%; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-104" style="padding-left: 0.303em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.264em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.72em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-105" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">Z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.547em 1000.42em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.321em; left: 0.784em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-106"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-107"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-108" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">∗</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.366em 1000.6em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -3.661em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-109"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-110"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-111" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">A</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.463em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.337em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-7">Z_{\rm L}(t)=Z_{\rm A}^{\ast}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> maximizes the power received by the passive tag. In the return (tag-to-reader) link, however, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-112" role="math" style="width: 2.646em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.345em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1002.23em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-113"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-114"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.204em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.72em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-115" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">Z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-116"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-117"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-118" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">L</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-119" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-120" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">t</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-121" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-8">Z_{\rm L}(t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula> controlled by the tag generates desired backscatter signals.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b"><h3>B. Transmit Diversity for a Passive UHF RFID Tag</h3><p>A work by Alamouti proposed a simple transmit diversity technique, which opens a new era of wireless communications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_2b">[7]</a>. On the transmitter end, the technique uses two antennas to transmit a pair of simple space-time encoded signal simultaneously. On the receiver end, a single antenna combines signals received from the two transmitter antennas to achieve the optimal decision of the transmitted signals. The technique is very suitable for wireless communication through a fading channel. This letter proposes an RFID tag with two antennas that have mutually orthogonal polarizations. The MBS in each antenna is polarization-time encoded.</p><p>It is noteworthy that some commercial tags use a cross-polarization antenna design to receive reader signals and send MBS in two polarizations <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_2b">[8]</a>. Such a tag can collect reader signal power from both polarizations in the forward link. However, without any preprocessing (or precoding) but merely sending the same MBS from both antennas, such a tag cannot fully exploit transmit diversity in the reverse link <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_2b">[9]</a>. Therefore, it is necessary to apply coding techniques to backscatter signals in both antennas to exploit transmit diversity in the reverse link.</p><p>In order to take the advantages of polarization diversity without overly increasing complexity, the tag design uses simple diversity-combining techniques. In the forward link, a reader signal received by the two tag antennas is obtained using selective combining technique; in the return link, encoded MBS can be backscattered from both antennas by controlling their corresponding impedance matching.</p><p>Considering tag computational ability, a simple Alamouti-like backscatter modulation method is applied so that each antenna only requires three different load impedances. In the forward link, the load impedance in each antenna is designed to be conjugate matching to the antenna impedance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-9-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-122" role="math" style="width: 5.168em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 4.628em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1004.51em 2.766em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-123"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-124" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-125"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.204em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.72em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-126" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">Z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-127"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-128"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-129" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">L</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-130" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-131" style="padding-left: 0.303em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.264em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.72em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-132" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">Z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.547em 1000.42em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.321em; left: 0.784em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-133"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-134"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-135" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">∗</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.366em 1000.6em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -3.661em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-136"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-137"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-138" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">A</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-139" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.463em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.337em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-9">(Z_{\rm L}=Z_{\rm A}^{\ast})</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, so the tag can harvest the maximal reader signal power from both antennas. In the return link, the load impedances of the two antennas are designed to generate an MBS with transmit diversity (i.e., backscatter diversity). Meanwhile, the received MBSs are combined at the receiver (reader). Since complicated processes, such as channel estimation and diversity combing, will be performed in the receiver end (i.e., a corresponding RFID reader), the complexity increment in the tag is insignificant.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2c"><h3>C. Load Impedances for a Dual-Antenna Passive UHF RFID Tag</h3><p>The MBS is determined by the reflection coefficient <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-10-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-140" role="math" style="width: 0.664em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1000.54em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-141"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-142" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Γ</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-10">\Gamma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, which is a function of the load impedance <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-11-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-143" role="math" style="width: 1.324em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.204em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1001.2em 2.465em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-144"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-145"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.204em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.72em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-146" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">Z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.664em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-147"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-148"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-149" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">L</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.197em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-11">Z_{\rm L}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> depicted in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1" href="#deqn1" class="fulltext-link">(1)</a>. Thus, a tag can send its signal to the reader by changing the load impedance of a tag antenna. The passive UHF RFID tag design in this letter has two antennas. The signals transmitted from the two antennas are polarization-time encoded (<a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>). As shown, unlike the original space-time coding table in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_2c">[7]</a> (<a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>), the tag backscatter encoding can reduce the requirement of different load impedances without compromising any diversity combining performance. Moreover, using two different load impedances rather than four also reduces the MBS detection errors.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>Encoding and Transmission Sequence<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>

Additionally, the two antennas backscatter different signals simultaneously (<a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a>), therefore the signals can be clearly separated.
</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table II </b>Proposed Backscatter Encoding Table<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-2-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p><a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a> shows that only two different MBS types (i.e., two different reflection coefficients) must be sent to each antenna. <div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-3-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p>It is noteworthy that the proposed polarization-time coding has two advantages. First, it simplifies the RFID tag design because each antenna only requires three different impedance matching states (one complex conjugate matching and two different antenna mismatches) instead of five (one complex conjugate matching and four different antenna mismatches). Second, each antenna only generates two different MBS instead of four, therefore the MBS can be clearly identified. This is particularly useful when a reader's operating bandwidth is wide (for instance, frequency-hopping cases) because reflection coefficients vary as the reader carrier frequency changes. Under such a circumstance, identifying two MBSs with opposite phases is much easier than identifying four MBSs with quadrature phase difference.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Experimental Results</h2></div><p><a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a> shows a dual-antenna passive UHF tag emulator including a Texas Instruments MSP430 microcontroller and two antenna control modules. Each antenna control module connects a dipole antenna (impedance 50 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-34-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-622" role="math" style="width: 0.844em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.66em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-623"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-624" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Ω</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-34">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at 900 MHz). Two commercial antennas made by Pulse Antennas are selected to demonstrate the generality of the proposed design <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_3">[10]</a>. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a> shows the schematic of each antenna control module. Note that because the emulator is battery-powered, the MSP430 microcontroller can emulate tag functions, such as demodulating reader signals, controlling backscatter signal modulators, and processing communication protocols between the emulator and a reader. Although the power harvester of a passive UHF tag is not included in the emulator, obviously the power harvester in a dual-antenna passive UHF tag can gain more reader power compared to a tag with a signal antenna (assuming that the same antenna is used). Therefore, some read range extension of the dual-antenna tag is expected in the forward link. Measuring the read range extension in the reverse link requires a reader that can combine backscatter signals received from the two antennas. Because such a reader is currently unavailable, the read range extension measurement is beyond the scope of this work.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>Photograph of a dual-antenna passive UHF tag emulator.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5765651/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>Schematic of each antenna control module.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5765651/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full table" id="table4"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table IV </b>Reflection Coefficients Corresponding to the Backscatter Signals with Transmit Diversity at Different Frequencies<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-table-4-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><p>The reflection coefficient for each antenna is designed according to <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a>, where the magnitude <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-35-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-625" role="math" style="width: 1.324em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.204em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1001.08em 2.646em -999.997em); width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-35">\vert\Gamma\vert</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and the phase <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-630" role="math" style="width: 0.544em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.264em 1000.48em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-631"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-632" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">θ</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-36">\theta</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the reflection coefficient at 900 MHz are designed to be 2/3 and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-633" role="math" style="width: 1.745em; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-636"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-637"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-638" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">/</span></span></span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-639" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">4</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-37">\pi/4</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, respectively. This design enables a passive tag to harvest partial power of an incident signal to maintain its minimal power requirement in the return link. <a ref-type="table" anchor="table4" class="fulltext-link">Table IV</a> shows measured reflection coefficient data for other frequencies. Note that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-38-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-640" role="math" style="width: 1.625em; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-39">i</script></tex-math></inline-formula>th antenna with source bit <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-40-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-652" role="math" style="width: 0.483em; The reflection coefficient variations for different frequencies are insignificant, which means that the proposed design can be used in an RFID system using a frequency-hopping scheme. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a> compares the measured reflection coefficients to the theoretical values obtained by the emulator, which confirms the feasibility of the proposed design.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>Comparison of reflection coefficients. <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-41-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-655" role="math" style="width: 2.384em; <div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>Measurement setup for dual-antenna passive UHF tag emulator test in an anechoic chamber.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5765651/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Snapshot of experiment for the dual-antenna passive UHF tag emulator.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5765651/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/5730210/5765651/5765651-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>Measured backscatter signal in time domain (upper part), where antenna 0 backscatters first and the antenna 1 follows. The corresponding constellation plot is shown in lower part.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/5765651/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Measurements of the dual-antenna passive UHF tag emulator are taken in an anechoic chamber at the National Taiwan University of Science and Technology. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a> illustrates the measurement setup, and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a> shows a snapshot of the experiments. Two identical circularly polarized patch antennas are connected to the transmitter (Tx) and the receiver (Rx), respectively. The tag emulator is about 1.5 m away. An Agilent signal generator transmits a reader command to the tag emulator. The tag emulator backscatters its polarization-time coded signals after it receives the reader command. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6</a> shows the backscatter signals in the time domain and their corresponding constellation plot recorded by an Agilent vector signal analyzer. To clearly illustrate the backscatter signals from the two dipole antennas independently, the tag emulator sends some MBSs from antenna 0 first (antenna 1 is matching at this moment), and then sends some MBSs from antenna 1 (antenna 0 is matching). This sequence can serve as pilot signals for channel estimation, which can be used for gain compensation and phase rotation in signal demodulation at a reader to overcome the constellation distortion problem discussed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_3">[11]</a>.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>As the power requirements of a passive UHF RFID tag power decrease, tag read range increases. However, the multipath fading effect remains problematic in a passive UHF RFID system. This letter proposes a tag with polarization-time coded backscatter diversity, which can be used to mitigate the fading channel problem. In the forward link, the tag uses a selective combining scheme to improve received reader signal quality. In the return link, the tag creates a transmit polarization diversity by using a simple encoding scheme and its corresponding load impedance change. Therefore, link budgets in both the forward link and the return link are reduced. Tags with dual dipoles are already available <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_4">[8]</a>. With the proposed tag chip design, these tags can exploit backscatter diversity. It is noteworthy that not only can the proposed design apply to a passive tag, but it also is valid for a semipassive tag.</p></div>
</div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

	if(getCookie('legacyUserName')!=null)
	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>
